0061452068|5.0|The newest book, The Rational Optimist, by one of the most interesting extant authors, a biologist turned to a kind of specialised, highly informed journalism. The kind of thing you love to read. (The Red Queen, Origins of Virtue, Genome, Nature via Nurture).<br />This book elaborates more on subjects touched in his Origins of Virtue, which I found his least convincing book, which is still very convincing. He does an even better job here.<br /><br />His main thesis is that exchange, of ideas (\\"Ideas having sex\\", not a metaphore, but an astute observation), and of goods and services (but not assets and capital) create progress and prosperity, and that specialisation, division of labour, is a necessary condition. A modern Adam Smith, if you like, but even more encompassing.<br /><br />He argues convincingly that trade was the cause of agriculture and the the human cultural 'take off' . He gives a very plausible -and as far as I know the only-  explanation of the technological regress found in isolated societies such as the Tasmanians, which in turn strengthens his argument.<br />He also shows convincingly that we have been doing better all the time in increased life expectancy, reduced violence (the latter subject more extensively treated by Steven Pinker's \\"The Better Angels of our Nature\\") and prosperity.<br />He opens our eyes to the benefits of the industrial revolution and acknowledges its precursors (eg, the Dutch peat fueled trading prosperity -one would like to use 'empire\\", but he makes it clear it was the opposite of an empire). From human power to animal and plant power to dead (fossil) animal and plant power.<br />He also makes it exquisitly clear that 'self sufficiency' and subsistence farming are about synonymous with poverty. No place for 'back to the mud'.<br /><br />His treatment of the environmental movement is highly critical, but balanced. He clearly is an 'environmentalist' himself.<br />His analysis of biofuels should be read by any politician, administrator, minister, director, CEO and journalist. I knew biofuels was a bad idea, but he gave me a lot of extra ammunition.<br />His  treatment of 'Global Warming'  is a bit, how should i say it, irresponsible?, No that sounds too harsh, optimistic? Nevertheless, one should wholeheartedly agree when he says: \\"But the threats to species are all to prosaic: habitat loss, pollution, invasive competitors and hunting are the same four horsemen of the ecological apocalypse as always. Suddenly many of the big environmental organisations have lost interest in these threats as they chase the illusion of stabilising a climate that was never stable in the past. It is as if the recent emphasis on climate change has sucked the oxygen from the conservation movement.\\" Oh Yes.<br /><br />His treatment of \\"turningpointitis\\" is masterful and balanced, acid rain, exhaustion of resources, environmentally caused cancer, and even the demographic explosion dealt with in an always interesting, but decisive way.<br /><br />There are some negative points,. Although I agree that much of innovation starts in the backyard garage, in his generally justified zeal to condemn bureaucracy, state and big enterprises, he fails to mention that some of these big state sponsored projects paid off. The search for the atomic bomb -well, the knowledge acquired there-, the human genome project, the Hubble Telescope or CERN are but some examples. To be fair he does admit that the hadron collider and moon missions are too big for private companies.<br /><br />I find it somewhat strange, particularly for somebody talking about 'ideas having sex', that he does not go into memetics. Left to the imagination of the reader I guess?<br /><br />His references, agreeably ordered by page and generally sound, fail to refer some important points. Eg.' there is no decrease in sperm count'. No reference. I checked it out and indeed there is no real decrease, but I think these things should be referenced properly.<br /><br />Another negative, in fact my biggest chip, are the graphs at the start of each chapter. They are adorned with irritating,  completely unnecessary illustrations that cloud the visual of the graph. I guess that is not the author's fault, but the editor should be crucified. One of the most interesting graphs, the \\"Greenland Ice Cap Temperature from Ice Cores' stops in the year 0, about 2000 years ago, and hence omitting the part we're most interested in.<br /><br />I've been thinking of only giving 4 stars for these serious shortcomings, but this book should be read, and it is a fascinating, controversial and an agreeable read, since Ridley is not just presenting important ideas, but is a stylist of the highest order, hence 5 stars.<br />Moreover, maybe I'm not yet a Rational Optimist, but I'm definitely less pessimistic after reading this book. Who am I to grudge that fifth star?

B0068MNO4S|2.0|Okay, I am a bit of a bardophile, so I was looking forward to watching this.  I like to see the bard and his works talked about from any number of different angles.  But if this movie had been a stool, it would be missing all but one leg.  The images were compelling and the other visual historical details were compelling (excepting visual historical mistakes, such as the singed cover of \\"Henry V\\" from the ashes of the theater, because the play had been performed before Edward's death).  Otherwise, the movie was a lumbering, clumsy, vindictive, pile of preposterousness.  God only knows why the anti-Shakespearean theories continue (although James Shapiro attempts to explain that in his book, \\"Contested Will\\"), but only deluded conspirators could find much to love in this.  The evidence to support Shakespeare is considerable and beats anything that I have seen from the opposition.  Still, this movie would just have been little more than a conspirator's delight had the movie leaked so much internally.<br /><br />For instance, Derek Jacobi, the actor, tells the audience that William had only a grammar school education.  But then the film portrays him as illiterate.  So which is it?  And of course, we can historically document the first claim, but the not the second.  What purpose would the claim of illiteracy (and to portray Shakespeare as a feckless, money-grubbing buffoon, again, without evidence) serve except to rev up the base of true believers.  If you're in a battle, it isn't enough to defeat your opponent; you must humiliate him as wel...<br /><br />It also made Queen Elizabeth -- by all accounts a brilliant and cunning leader -- a credulous pawn of the Cecils, and blinkered ruler in general.  And yet again, the producers employed the humiliation strategy, coming with a preposterous and completely unfounded calumny toward the queen and pitiful William.  Do the producers have Oedipal issues, perhaps?  It also doesn't make sense that the Earl would give into Shakespeare's blackmail either.  Isn't the Earl supposed to be so much more clever than anyone else?  Then why would he consent to pay WS?  WS has no bargaining leverage to speak of.  If he does what he threatens, he would lose everything (probably including his life).  This is Elizabethan England.  The Earl could have WS vanish, mafia-like, in no time and then simply find someone else to replace him (it was clear from the movie: any idiot could have done the job).<br /><br />The acting was also unconvincing.  Was any audience member able to see the Earl as the author of some of the most beautiful, heartfelt prose in the world, especially the most romantic and sublime verses?  Moreover, a key assumption that the antishakespeareans make is that one can only write what one knows (a literary fashion mistake from the Romantic period); so, it goes, WS was not high enough born and well enough connected to know so much about kings and courts.  Somehow the Earl of Oxford fits this bill better.  But then the Oxfordians put the noose around their own necks with the charge, because WS ALSO wrote many characters from the peasant side -- and many in the middle.  How could that noble Earl know so much about them?<br /><br />It's sad that this type of mularkey continues, with little more than wild speculation and willed blindness to evidence to keep it going, and that so many credulous have been pulled in.  Okay, it deserves as much of a place in the public conversation as creationism, anti-global warming, and Obama birthplace debates.  Crackpot ideas will be around as long as there are crackpots.  But this film isn't even for them!

B007SAKSF2|5.0|\\"THIS IS THE NEXT CENTURY....\\"<br /><br />Whenever a box set comes out, I approach it with caution. Let's face it, these things can be costly. However, let's really think about what you're getting here. Over the past three to five years, we've seen box sets around this price that are commemorating JUST ONE SINGLE ALBUM! Pearl Jam \\"Ten\\", The Who's Quadrophenia, etc. However, here you have 97% of their output... in beautifully designed packages, and the sound is spectacular. So, in this review, I'll discuss the content, and try to silence the skeptics.<br /><br />First, I've read a lot of \\"But I already have all this stuff. Why would you need it?\\"<br /><br />A - Standards, first of all. If you are a casual fan and don't care about the bonus material, this isn't targeted at you anyway. Second, if you primarily listen to music on the free earbuds that come with your iPhone, well, you may not care about remastering. HOWEVER, for those of you that love music and love Blur and have standards, the remastering came out spectacularly!<br /><br />Frank Arkwright is AGAINST the Loudness Wars and he does NOT \\"Just make things louder\\". He sonically improves things. He did a fantastic job with The Smiths' catalogue, and has done it again with this one! It's not just louder.<br /><br />PLEASE LET ME EXPLAIN WHAT REMASTERING IS:  They take the original MASTER tapes, and use MODERN 2012 technology to create a new master. That's what happens when you do it properly. Frank Arkwright is an engineer who does original masters too. He knows this stuff. When the artist or producer isn't involved, and when they hire some unqualified record company schlub to remaster, you may get them simply running a CD mix through an equalizer and pumping up the volume. This  isn't the case here. They remastered the songs from the original master tapes. The albums say they're remastered - the demos and bonuses don't specify it. However, most of the bonus material is being mastered for the first time, rght?Make sense? Well, last thing, remastering is NOT remixing! They're not remixing anything! I don't know why people don't get this. This isn't a remix. The sonic improvement is great!<br /><br />You get all of the albums in their original UK release form. This means, the UK version of \\"LEISURE\\" had the track \\"Sing\\", so it's included here on the album, but did not contain \\"I Know\\", so it isn't included on CD 1. Instead, it's included on the bonus CD. Same goes for \\"Modern Life Is Rubbish\\". In the U.S., ours had bonus tracks like the Single-only release \\"POPSCENE\\". Like \\"I Know\\", \\"Popscene\\" is now on the Bonus disc. Also important to point out is the fact that the version of \\"Chemical World\\" we had in the U.S. was never the original UK version. It was a re-recording for the U.S. I actually preferred this re-recorded version, and the vocal is completely different. But it isn't included here. You get the original U.K. album version and the UK single \\"Re-worked version\\".<br /><br />Apart from these details, it all sounds wonderful. Songs like \\"Fade Away\\", \\"Best Days\\" and \\"Yuko and Hiro\\", all from THE GREAT ESCAPE, have all been given new life. The breaking bottles that open the track \\"Parklife\\" appear as though they're breaking around your feet. Does it sound like that on your 1\\" HP Computer speakers? How could it? Your crappy free iPhone earbuds? No. On GOOD speakers? It's fantastic!<br /><br />You don't get EVERYTHING, like The Who cover \\"Substitute\\", but... you get all you'd ever want as even the most diehard fan.<br /><br />Some more specifics about the box:<br />The box is a fabric-covered, sturdy box that perfectly fits all of the albums and bonus discs. Each album is presented in a Mini-LP Digipak CD with Disc one in one end, and Disc two (Bonuses) in the other side. These are beautiful. You get two bonus 2-CD albums of Rarities from each Era. Example, CD 1: Seymour and Leisure Rarities, CD 2, Modern Life Is Rubbish Rarities - and so forth. The final bonus RaritiesCD includes \\"Fool's Day\\" and \\"Under The Westway\\". You get 3 DVDs that cover 3 live shows from different eras of the band. These are great to have and are not available individually. These DVDs are all REGION 0, NTSC.<br /><br />Let me be clear: there is just one edition of this box. It's on FOOD/Parlophone Records (EMI). There isn't a risk of the DVDs not playing in your country. The DVD isn't formatted with a region restriction. They work on ANY Player, whether cheap or expensive, on your PS3 too, wherever you may be in the world. They look and sound great and they do NOT have a UK version in PAL, and a U.S. version in NTSC. NTSC works in the UK and everywhere else. You're not limited to watching DVDs on your computer.<br /><br />You get the Seymour 7\\" single \\"Superman\\" inserted nearly in the box alongside a sturdy, full-color 7\\" x 7\\" hardback book. You also get a download code to download all of these songs (except \\"Superman\\" curiously) in 320kbps MP3 form, and you can download bonus songs and PDFs and even get a 10% off discount code from the Blur shop so you can buy that expensive yet cool throwback t-shirt you once impressed the girls with in the 90's. They have new shirts and stuff, too.<br /><br />This set is essential for any Blur fan. You already have this stuff? Upgrade! The sound and bonuses are well worth it. Trade those old crappy versions of the CDs you have into your local record shop and apply it to your favorite Blur Vinyl Re-issue. Those, too, are gorgeous. The NEW CDs in this box look identical to the original issues, with the Food/Parlophone logo on each. The CD covers too have those logos (It's NOT on Virgin. My box says Virgin nowhere on it.)<br /><br />If you truly are a Blur fan, this is well worth the dough! They were uniquely British and that served me well in the 90s when everyone was trying so hard to sound grunge/American. I'm proud to be American, but give me some originality! Were they the best British Band? It's tough to put them above early Suede or 90s PULP, but they're incredibly inventive and amongst the best ever - and if you feel this way about Graham and Damon and the boys, you will LOVE this set.<br /><br />A side note: the individual CD Re-issues come in a bonus lift-off box and postcards that have each single's artwork. This box, in order to fit everything, does NOT include that bonus box or postcards. However, I don't miss it and would rather have the DVDs and Rarities discs that aren't available separately!<br /><br />FINALLY, on a very limited basis, there's an Incredible FREE, yes FREE, BLUR app for the iPad and iPhone. Grab it while you can! It has interviews for all of the albums and eras. The videos show them in 1992, and throughout the years, discussing each album, and interviews from NOW too. It also includes EVERY Blur music video! All for FREE!  It's a great time to be a Blur fan...

0374203032|5.0|I came to this book without any foreknowledge on the issues raised or of the author's previous work. The brief description on Amazon's web site grabbed my interest. The author of this book is Michael Sandel, he is a professor of politics at Harvard; his first worldwide hit was with his last book, Justice, the subject of a famous lecture course at Harvard. His new book, What Money Can't Buy, is a study of &#34;the moral limits of markets&#34;.<br /><br />Call me nave and uninformed, but as delved into the book I began to find some rather disturbing facts, I never realised that organizations such as Wal-Mart had taken out hundreds of thousands of insurance policies on employees, where they were the beneficiary, so every time one of these employees' died, Walmart enjoyed a bonus. This type of policy was better known in the media as dead peasants insurance. These policies are forms of what the insurance industry calls Stoli, or &#34;stranger originated life insurance&#34; - in other words, an insurance policy taken out on your life by someone else, not on your behalf but on theirs.<br /><br />Another type of insurance that was initially very popular with investors was the creation and evolution of `viaticals', and quote from Wiki `The early victims of AIDS in the U.S. were largely gay men, many of whom were not particularly old. They often had no wives or children (the traditional beneficiaries under a life insurance policy), but they had life insurance policies through employment or as a result of investments. The beneficiaries under the policies were often their parents who did not need the money. Viatical settlements offered a way to extract value from the policy while the policy owner was still alive' to pay for care and medical treatment. In the early years, of the disease being identified, life expectancy for people with full blown AIDS was very low, so investors did very well. These are just some the examples where Markets have evolved since the 1980s.<br /><br />Sandel work is both focused and paced; there is this no ideological bias or political tilt in his writing. Rather<br />Sandel writes in his book, &#34;Markets - and market values - have come to govern out lives as never before.&#34; Do we in society wish to see markets, where everything is up for sale? Or should there be areas, where there are certain moral and community goods/services, where markets forces do not apply and where money should not be able to buy. For me it was a thought provoking read and somewhat of an eye opener, where we need to take interest in the ways markets are focused and developed./>Sandel work is both focused and paced; there is this no ideological bias or political tilt in his writing. 0375704027|3.0|Murakami is known for his odd, quirky characters and his affinity for talking cats, but Norwegian Wood is a departure from that. Yes, the characters are quirky but probably the least quirky I've encountered thus far and I've read eight of his books in the past year and a half.<br /><br />Essentially, the book functions as a love story. At its center is Toru Watanabe. He's an average guy and a decent student. While at college, he befriends Kizuki and Naoko who happen to be dating but the two of them are not complete without the addition of Toru's friendship. All three of them acknowledge this at some point in their relationship, yet when Kizuki dies tragically, Naoko and Toru remain friends, but their friendship is challenged by Naoko's inability to function without Kizuki. This forces her to spend some time away, recuperating from her sadness.<br /><br />While away, Toru goes about his life as he normally would trying to figure out where he stands with Naoko and then in walks Midori. Midori has her own issues and although the two take comfort in each other's company, they can't seem to move past the Toru/Naoko connection. What starts off as an innocent friendship turns into something else, but how far can it go when your heart also loves another?<br /><br />My reaction to the book may have been due to the translation but the writing was simplistic to me. Overly so, and that's not something I expect while reading a Murakami novel. The dialogue was stilted and almost seemed forced in some places. At first, I enjoyed the slowness of it, but when the dialogue continued this way, I began to get frustrated with it. It really played out as a \\"He Said, She Said\\" and its predictability in both plot and pattern nearly put me to sleep at one point. But, there are telltale signs of Murakami's familiar style too which is probably why I continued reading. His characters are always so interesting even if what they had to say wasn't.<br /><br />However, there was a \\"creep\\" factor to this novel that I've not experienced with any of Murakami's other books. The \\"relations\\" between some of the characters set my teeth on edge. Many have said this is one of Murakami's more erotic novels but I didn't find it to be overly erotic or graphic. However, I did feel uncomfortable numerous times while reading it. The conversations about sex just didn't seem realistic me. You wouldn't walk up to a friend and say, \\"Hey, it would be nice to see your penis just to see how impressive it is. Don't you think?\\" Not a line from the book but it's a good example of what I am talking about. Polite and smutty all at the same time.<br /><br />Overall, I enjoyed the musical references and listened to Norwegian Wood (by the Beatles) a few times while reading but the story was very slow and the high creep factor turned me off. Not one of my favorites, but I suspect that readers who do not appreciate the surreal quality of his other novels, might prefer the straight-forwardness of this one.o this novel that I've not experienced with any of Murakami's other books. B000IZ1HP4|5.0|For cult western enthusiasts, I'll give this a 5-star rating.  I saw this 1968 film originally in theatre and was blown away with its idealized and romanticized portrayal of the old west with huge cast and production values.  The first-time viewing of director Servio Leone's (spaghetti western fame) intentional slow pace & subtle humor of this epic will likely make you think you wasted your money.  But watch it a couple more times, plus the included behind-the-scenes, and you'll begin to appreciate the inverted effort to most other same-period American shot films: i.e, like the equally good 1969 Wild Bunch---where there's normal pacing with ultra-slow motion shoot-outs & blood spurting everywhere.  Then you'll begin to grasp that the 'dance' of the duals are time-elongated (as if you were there at the depot, swatting flies yourself), with realistic 1/10 sec. shootout resolutions.  And once establishing shots of actors physicality determined, why not just do ultra close-ups of their gritty, sweaty expressions of famous actors like Henry Fonda and Charles Bronson, lathered horses, etc.?  It also has great sound and sparse dialog, as the faces and action tell the story much better.

031620157X|4.0|I am ashamed to admit that The Land of Stories: The Wishing Spell would probably not have been on my radar if Chris Colfer hadn't written it. I might have picked it up at the library and checked it out, or contemplated buying it for a few dollars at Half Price Books, but I never would have ordered an autographed copy from Barnes & Noble in NYC, spending $23.99 and several shaky, social-anxiety filled minutes on the phone in the process.<br /><br />They say not to judge a book by its cover, and yet, if Chris Colfer hadn't written The Land of Stories, the only thing that would have drawn me to it would have been its cover. It's a children's book- eight to twelve, I've heard people say- and I've long since passed the children's section in the library. But if I had run across The Wishing Spell in a library by mistake, I might have checked it out on the cover alone. It's gorgeous, beautifully illustrated, and it captures the essence of the story perfectly. In fact, as I'm writing this, I keep looking over at it wistfully, running my hand across the spine. It's that pretty.<br /><br />But this book was written by Chris Colfer. So I did order a signed copy, even though it wasn't written for my age range, and I did spend several hours stroking the pretty book jacket longingly when I first bought it, though that might not have been because of Brandon Dorman's beautiful artwork but rather because of the big fat \\"Autographed Copy\\" sticker on the front cover. Writing a book review for a story penned by my idol makes me slightly nervous, because I'm a little bit unsure of whether or not I'm letting my fangirling influence my opinions on the book, but I'm going to try to be as honest as possible.<br /><br />The Wishing Spell follows twelve-year-old twins Alex and Conner Bailey on a fractured fairy tale adventure. After falling into their grandmother's cherished fairy tale book, they find themselves in the 'Land of Stories'; a place where fairies, elves, trolls, goblins, peasants,and royalty alike all live. They meet famous characters such as Prince Charming (all four of them), Cinderella, Sleeping Beauty, Snow White, Rapunzel, Red Riding Hood, Goldilocks, and even the Little Mermaid (in her real, not Disney-fied form: sea foam). They travel far and wide to gather the items necessary to complete the Wishing Spell, a magic spell that will supposedly take them home.<br /><br />One thing that I know for sure is that this book wouldn't have been as meaningful for me if it hadn't been written by Chris Colfer, because I wouldn't have made such an ordeal of it- savoring each page, crying when it's over, thinking to myself, hey, Chris Colfer grew up with a special needs sister, was bullied to the moon and back, and almost attended community college because he had nowhere else to go, but now he's an award-winning actor and has written a New York Times Bestseller, so suck on that, haters. I don't think I would have been as moved if Chris Colfer hadn't written it, and that is hard to admit. But knowing Colfer's back story actually adds to the story in ways that I hadn't thought possible. There is advice in the story that makes more sense when the author has a name and a face and a history. When the twins are talking to Cinderella, for example- \\"'I never thought my life would change so drastically, so I always made the most of what I had,' Cinderella said. 'I always laugh at the term Cinderella story, because, if you ask me, it doesn't matter what life you're living, life never has a solution. No matter how hard the struggles are that you leave behind, new struggles always take their place.'\\" (page 190)- it's almost as if Colfer is talking about his life through the characters, and I think that's the beauty of reading a story written by somebody you know and love dearly. You understand what the story means to the author as well as what it means to the reader. I would have thought that these small lessons would seem slightly transparent- they definitelyare when isolated from the story-, but they were actually placed quite delicately, in a way that didn't seem pompous or arrogant, and discovering them was a sort of serendipity. (\\"Froggy leaned down beside her. 'It doesn't matter how greatly you've been hurt or how much you're hurting, it's what you do with the pain that counts,' he said. 'You could cry for years, and rightfully so, or you could choose to learn and grow from it. Take it from me: I spent years hiding in a hole, afraid to come out because of what people would think of me. But one day I decided to leave, and I ended up saving lives!'\\" page 410). It was almost like getting a hug from the author himself.<br /><br />Despite him being both my role model and celebrity crush, I can't sugarcoat it: Chris Colfer is not a perfect writer. Most of his sentences were of the same length (which is to say, quite short) and some of them were clunky. He didn't eloquently weave his words and his diction didn't leave me breathless. Although there were a couple similes that I chuckled at (\\"Conner's mouth hung open like a broken glove compartment.\\" page 50), the book was painfully free of figurative language, and there were far too many clichs for my liking. A lot of this could be chalked up to the fact that it's a children's book (and sadly, I don't think every eight-year-old is as concerned about great writing as I was at that age), but I also get the feeling that this is just the type of writer Chris Colfer is: fairly straightforward, more focused on the plot than the writing itself. He actually reminds me of J.K. Rowling, in that he is more of a storyteller than writer (and I mean absolutely no offense with that- they're both just more interested in telling a beautiful story rather than crafting beautiful sentences). His writing style leads me to believe that he may be a better screenwriter than novel-writer, because his strengths are in plot and dialogue.<br /><br />Although it's been done hundreds of times, ChrisColfer had a fresh and original take on fractured fairy tales. I was slightly disappointed in the first couple hundred pages because most of his plot 'twists' were incredibly predictable and because many of the 'obstacles' the twins faced were solved almost immediately. It all seemed incredibly convenient until perhaps the last hundred pages or so. I'm usually pretty obtuse when it comes to plot twists (unfortunately, I could never be a detective), but there were two or three surprises at the end that kept me on my toes (and forced me to stick my fist over my foolish grin and stop myself from laughing). Perhaps somebody more observant or clever than I am could have predicted the ending(s), but it pleased me well enough, and after finishing the novel I found it more obvious that the plot \\"twists\\" in the beginning/middle of the book were supposed to be predicted and anticipated (again... I'm kind of obtuse). The characters were well-developed for a children's book (they were quite stereotyped, but it is written for eight to twelve year olds), and even Conner, who was mostly there for comic relief, had a couple of very touching moments. All in all, the story was pretty well done, and the ending, which was messy and complicated and somehow wonderful as well, made me shed a few tears. It was definitely my favorite part, and I didn't realize how invested I had become in the characters and the story until I turned the last page.<br /><br />Now, one last point before I wrap up this novel-length review: I was so, so, so pleased to find that Chris Colfer wrote a book that not only had strong, important female characters, but was even centered around these strong female characters. I can only think of three or four male characters that played as much significance (with dozens of characters, that was quite a surprise), and it was so refreshing, especially for the fairy tale genre, a genre that is often criticized by feminists for depicting the man as the hero and woman as the damsel in distress. Even the typically dull female princesses, such as Cinderella and Sleeping Beauty, were written as brave and strong, with far more personality and wisdom than they've ever had in the original fairy tales. Chris wasn't as progressive, however, when it came to POCs- by my count, there was only one black character, and she was quite minor. I'm sure he didn't mean to do it, and I'm sure part of it was that he wanted to stick to the original fairy tale characters and their original physical traits, but it was another depressing example of white privilege, and one that was pretty hard to ignore.<br /><br />So: Did I like the book? Yes. I loved it. Is it because Chris Colfer wrote it? No. Absolutely not. Was I impressed/astounded/wow-ed beyond belief? Yes, especially at the end. Is it because Chris Colfer wrote it? Hmm... Probably? You read it, and tell me how you feel.<br /><br />If there's one thing I've learned from The Wishing Spell, it's that there's always more to a story, even after 'happily ever after'.<br /><br />I can't wait for the sequel.<br /><br />TLDR; OK/good for teens/adults, great for kids. Would recommend to everyone, partially because it's written by a fantastic person and partially because it's a great book. Keep in mind that it's a children's book as you're reading it.<br /><br />To see more reviews visit littlecricketreviews.blogspot.com :)

A man whose known film making style is to put implausible but superstitious material on screen for teenagers (and teenagers at heart) to soak up and enjoy?<br /><br />Well, I'm not a teenager anymore, thank goodness, and when I first heard about this film I was enthused to see what kind of story would be presented on the screen.  But, there were flags.  The actors at the press junket was a big one; \\"acting\\" (to coin a phrase) as if they were outraged that the material they were presenting had been \\"hidden\\" for so long by so few and accepted by so many (to borrow from another Brit, W. Churchill).  I really didn't think anything of it at the time, and merely fobbed it off as just more dramatics to sell the film.<br /><br />Then I see the film.  I give a collective \\"huh\\", and leave the theatre wondering if the entire crew and company think that those of us who doubt Shakespeare's literary veracity are merely a bunch of nut cases.<br /><br />But, you know what?  I don't care.<br /><br />William Shakespeare had lots of education and inherit intelligence.  He was not a dumb man.  But he did not write nor create the artistry in the so labelled \\"Shakespeare Portfolio\\".  His education was in commerce and legal procedures, it was not in the pass-times of Elizabethan's \\"Rich and Famous\\" (again, to coin a phrase).  Nor was it in astronomy, military matters, international relations, Mediterranean social morays, and a host of other things found in the Shakespearean portfolio that are needed in order to write those works.<br /><br />And, on the screen, it's even worse.  Instead of a Shakespeare who seems tohave been a family man in spite of his legal wrangles with other merchants, we're given a slovenly womanizer who extorts money from De Vere.<br /><br />The film goes on about court intrigue, who is sleeping with whom, and pretty much every paranoid fantasy that one could ever have about the people involved with those works at that time.<br /><br />It really amazes me that anyone would presume that because an individual doubted one facet of that era regarding \\"Shakespeare\\", that therefore said individual BELIEVED in ALL of the paranoid crap that was put into this movie.<br /><br />The Raisuli take on Shakeseare; he was a producer, and this seems to make sense as he had a lot of experience in dealing with people in a court of law, as well as dealing in money, notably grains.  He probably had a legal reputation that kept people  from suing him and his theatre troupe for libel and slander.  The works were probably produced under him as \\"the author\\" because he was the man bringing the resources to the stage, but nothing beyond that.<br /><br />How on Earth this manifests into riots and mass slaughters on the London bridge and elsewhere, and transpires into murders and cover ups is really beyond the scope of my imagination.<br /><br />But, some Over-The-Top German decides to produce a paranoid driven film, and \\"Anonymous\\" is what we get.<br /><br />It's truly a piece of well produced garbage.  See it once for the art direction.<br /><br />Otherwise trash it, or leave it on the shelf.  Your brain will thank you for the rest of your life.

B000059HB7|5.0|It's often said that the three greatest westerns are The Searchers, Rio Bravo, and Red River. While Searchers and Red River (big cattle drive type movie) are made on a grand scale, Rio Bravo is a smaller, more intimate film. Much of it actually takes place in-doors, especially in the small jail and sheriff's office where Walter Brennan and Ricky Nelson are often seen with Wayne just shootin' the bull and hanging out and singing the occasional cowboy tune.<br /><br />The film sports an all-star cast, including Dean Martin, Walter Brennan,  Ward Bond, Angie Dickinson, future TV star Claude Akins, and others. Wayne plays a more complex character here, not simply the uncomplicated macho-type with which he's usually associated, and not so different from his last movie, The Shootist, which was still many years in the future.<br /><br />The movie gets much of its appeal as well as drama from this more intimate style of story telling as the characters interact and get to know each other on several levels as the movie progresses. Martin does a great job as the recovering alcoholic deputy who's still fast with a gun. Ricky Nelson does a fine job in a supporting role as the young gunslinger. And Brennan is terrific as the handicapped but cantankerous old coot who's sticks by his friend Wayne through thick and thin.<br /><br />A little trivia here, Lauren Bacall's character in The Shootist, where she is referred to as \\"the widow, Bond,\\" is a reference to Ward Bond who was an omni-present character actor for decades and appeared in a number of Wayne films--26 if I remember right--the most of any actor. \\"Ward\\" was short for \\"Wardell\\" I recently found out. Wayne and Bond met in college playing football at the Univ. of Southern California, where they began a lifelong friendship and movie collaboration.<br /><br />With a fine script by Leigh Bracket (who would later achieve fame as a science fiction story writer) and score by Dmitri Tiomkin to round out the film's charms, it's a timeless classic from Howard Hawkes that is still worth watching today.eless classic from Howard Hawkes that is still worth watching today.

B005CRQ4XI|3.0|I had mixed reactions to this book.<br /><br />As a disclaimer, I would like to point out that I am not in the target audience for this book. I am 58 and male. I read the book because I am a backpacker. The book sells mostly to young, slim (probably athletic) women. Why do I make this assertion? I went to Cheryl Strayed's event and book-signing. 95% of the large audience (Ms. Strayed is a rock star) fit this target market. The other 5% probably came for the electronic, new-age musician.<br /><br />If I were in the target market, if I had identified more strongly with Ms. Strayed (or her 24-year old self), I would probably have loved this book. If you can identify with Cheryl Strayed, then you may love this book.<br /><br />If you cannot form this bond, you may dislike the book because of the follow reasons:<br /><br />1. The language and metaphors are fairly pedestrian. I kept thinking, I have heard that analogy or phrasing in many books (often self-help books, no accident that Ms. Strayed was a self-help columnist). The author usually avoids obvious cliches, but if you reflect upon media discussions that focus on personal growth, you will recognize most of the language. For example, the author loves the adverb, \\"profoundly.\\" She also uses some obvious tricks to make the writing seem compelling: sexual obscenities (not an objection for me, but more of an author tic) and exaggerating verbs -- \\"destroyed\\" for tired and \\"shattered\\" for distraught or depressed. Not terrible, but not Joan Didion or Dave Eggers.<br /><br />2. Cheryl Strayed likes metaphor as the primary tool in story-telling (call it approach A). She made this comment in the event that I attended. Many authors, however, focus upon precise, sensory detail to show depth of character, point of view, voice and story development. For these authors (call this approach B), focusing upon metaphor creates overwrought story-telling. Approach B is not a law (no laws in story-telling). I, however, like approach B. If you like approach A, then read the book. To be fair, the author did use a lot of good detail, but it was not the primary driver for the story.<br /><br />3. Almost all of the flashbacks to Strayed's last few years (of her drug-use, frantic-sex, relationship-destroying period) were linked to the on-the-trail narrative through metaphor. In addition to liking approach A, Strayed did not have much chance to really reflect or think about her mental or social problems on the trail. As she wrote, she was surviving -- wrestling with some incredible discomforts or worrying about some big problems (enough water or possibly woman-eating mountain lions). To weave her recent life problems into the narrative, Strayed, the writer, needed her thematic or metaphorical links. For example, coming upon a long, snowed-over section of the trail gives her an image and loose theme (snow and hardship) to write about how she had to shoot her beloved horse in the snow to euthanize the poor creature. That description would have stood better on its own; it was perhaps the most powerful mini-story in the book. It did not gain as a flashback from her trail problems. Moreover, the trail flow was broken unnecessarily by the horse story. She switched back and forth, through metaphor, repeatedly. Chopping up the PCT trail narrative this extensively diffused the trail tension. The metaphors were not strong or original enough to overcome the structural problems.<br /><br />If you, however, feel a deep, powerful identification with  the author, this structural point will probably not be a problem for you.<br /><br />4. While focusing upon metaphor, the author avoids some obvious problems and explanations: Her pre-trail use of heroin ballooned into an almost daily habit for months. People become strongly addicted when using heroin at this frequency. Ms. Strayed never mentions withdrawal after stopping the habit almost cold-turkey. If she had suffered withdrawal, it would have been a horrific experience.Why did she avoid mentioning that issue?<br /><br />Likewise, when she takes a serious fall, dropping down a rocky trail with her impossible-to-carry pack, she writes about the aftermath as if she had suffered a painful but superficial knee-scrape. Reality check: when I have suffered falls that severe on a trail, I have torn ligaments or cartilage, effectively ending my trip. It seems that Cheryl Strayed is blessed with extremely good luck after a number of ordinarily crippling events (high frequency injections of street heroin, falling hard down rocks with a 50 lbs. backpack crushing her, almost getting raped by some lunatic, getting her feet so blistered that most skin peeled off...). Are all the escapes and endurance-of-crippling-pain possible? Yes. If you are a Navy Seal. Memory is subjective, and any of us can enhance a story from repeated retelling.<br /><br />5. The author's pre-trail behavior (extreme impulsiveness) fits borderline personality disorder (BPD). This impulsiveness suggests much more than just Strayed's explanation -- grief. Grief could have triggered this mental illness, but not explained it. I can understand that Strayed did not want to crimp her character's possibilities with a psychiatric diagnosis. BPD, however, is a serious and real mental illness, and its treatment almost always means a much longer ordeal than a three month hike. Although a three month wilderness journey can be life-changing, it is almost never a cure for serious mental illness. Again, the author endures almost unbelievable mental and physical hardship, and never slows in her forward motion.<br /><br />Finally, what kept me reading was not the description of the wilderness (which was interesting), but rather my curiosity about how Cheryl Strayed would tie together all of her themes and potential solutions. She did it, characteristically, with some metaphors. (Crater Lake was a mountain with a heart torn out, that eventually healed -- like her.) As far as how she got herself together enough to stop life-threatening impulsiveness: inexplicable trail-magic transformed her -- somehow loosening the grief inside of her. (She wrote that the trail was not just a journey outside, but more of a journey inside. I think of literary types advising: show, don't tell. The words \\"inside\\" and \\"outside\\" are lackluster figures of speech and poor substitutes for \\"showing\\" descriptions of the internal or external.) I came away thinking that Strayed was waving some magic wands of metaphor, and not telling the whole story -- perhaps in many ways.<br /><br />The Kindle format -- one final point:<br /><br />The quotes from literature under the section headings on my Kindle (a new, basic version) are garbled. These same quotes in the print version are right justified, and are readable. The quotes in the Kindle version are crushed as a one-character column on the right margin and continue, scrambled, for multiple pages. They are unreadable.<br /><br />Amazon and readers need to do something about shoddy publishing products. We are paying $12.99 for this e-book. Readers need to demand more -- have an old iPad for example to buy a clean version if it is available. Also, protest when publishers deliver this crap. Likewise, Amazon needs to get publishers to deliver well-formatted ebooks. Or, in the long run, writers need to bypass these publishers.Why did she avoid mentioning that issue?<br /><br />Likewise, when she takes a serious fall, dropping down a rocky trail with her impossible-to-carry pack, she writes about the aftermath as if she had suffered a painful but superficial knee-scrape. 0374203032|4.0|Earlier reviewers have worked through most of the pros and cons of What Money Can't Buy. None, however, picks up on what to me is the single most stimulating sentence in the book. On p. 10 Sandel notes that we have never had a proper debate about the relations among moral, political, and economic issues. He continues: \\"As a result, without quite realizing it, without ever deciding to do so, we drifted from [being a society] having a market economy to being a market society.\\" The italics are Sandel's--the only authorial italics in the book--and the sentence is the underlying thesis of the book.<br /><br />Since Sandel develops his thesis anecdotally rather than analytically, it is useful expand on his italics. A society \\"having a market economy\\" is a situation in which some overarching value system manages relations among people (\\"society\\") and uses a market economy as a tool to manage economic affairs. \\"The market\\" is not an autonomous system that runs itself but a subordinate system managed by a combination of public and private managers. Those managers, in theory, would guide the economy according to the value system organizing social relationships. For the United States that value system is summarized in the second paragraph of the Declaration of Independence, beginning with the \\"self-evident truth\\" that \\"all [people] are created equal.\\"<br /><br />A \\"market society,\\" on the other hand, is a situation in which market forces embody both the overarching value system and the autonomous power to direct not only all economic function but also all relations among people. The Market, in free market ideology, is the dominant legitimizing force, providing the ultimate sanction for all relationships--economic, political, institutional, personal. It supplants the roles of government, religion, education, family, and tradition. It destroys the very idea of a legitimate public realm. It inexorably monetizes more and more of life. Money increasingly serves as the fundamental value on which all choices are based.<br /><br />And that is why the individual examples raised by Sandel are so disquieting: they reveal how the Market de-legitimizes all values but money.

B008645YEE|2.0|Minimalistic, like the last Zimmer scores. The whole soundtrack revolves around a chant, a two-note motif that never evolved from the first movie and a nice, yet casual, composition for catwoman that has no true emotions underneath.<br /><br />Zimmer has been towards the direction of minimalistic compositions, yet the truth is this compositions only sound great because of all the arrangements he does to those simple melodies. Zimmer is brilliant at arranging his simplistic melodies to achieve a sense of awe. Yet, to the experienced soundtrack listener, the complexity of his past compositions have been a bit mediocre, revolving always around two-note's: the main theme of the Last Samurai (in here it sounded fresh), the main theme heard at the end of The Da Vinci Code, the main theme heard at the end of Inception, the Batman theme... all main themes that are just two notes. Technically speaking, two notes are not a melody. Play the batman theme on a piano... come on, or a flute, a guitar perhaps?... it sounds really lazy.<br /><br />Now play the main theme from Schindler's List on piano, or the Hobbit's theme from Lord of the Rings, or the Braveheart theme... there are many fantastic themes that are just that: fantastic, beautiful, melodic, full of emotion and complexity. But what Zimmer does with his two-notes is wrap them around bombastic sound effects and really well done arrangements so that it sounds imposing. A different way of composing, yet I feel the amount of effort invested on this kind of compositions is less than the one invested in composing something truly melodic and complex that has much more than two notes... and they can also be imposing when the respective composers decide to lure them with orchestral arrangements.<br /><br />So, at the end, Zimmer does regular compositions with exceptional arrangments. You can take it or leave it but the truth is that (at least for his latest main themes). Put into other words, imagine soundtrack composers had thegift of designing faces; Zimmer creates average ordinary faces (visually speaking), but then he applies the proper make-up to them and, at the end, they appear to look beautiful for the casual eye. But if you take that make-up off, you'll find the truth. Other composers make beautiful faces from the beginning that look beautiful without the need of make-up (of course, they can also apply make up to those naturally-looking beautiful faces and create something you'll never forget).<br /><br />Four years have passed since the last Dark Knight score and Zimmer knew this soundtrack would be extremely anticipated. Trilogy scores are really difficult to pull off because you need to sound refreshing and unique in every episode without deviating too much from the familiar sound so that people still feel inside the same story. Howard Shore and John Williams did it with Lord of the Rings and Star Wars to a point of perfection, introducing new outstanding themes for every episode while still capturing the same essence of the story's atmosphere; in the last episode they where able to tie things up by reuniting all the main themes created throughout the trilogy and introducing new refreshing themes that were equally great.<br /><br />Zimmer bet everything on the past themes and composed two new theme's for the new characters but nothing new for the emotional final inner struggle of Bruce Wayne: this can be appreciated in the track \\"Rise\\"... just the same track of \\"A Dark Knight\\" with small pieces of Batman Begins and \\"I'm Not A Hero\\" glued in. Zimmer can do better, and we all know that.<br /><br />Hans Zimmer's new sound has been tilted towards the electronical side also; like a DJ remixing pieces of past compositions with new electronic sounds, Zimmer achieves the sound of a modern pop CD instead of a classical orchestral score, with the end result of a very unnatural sound for the listener.<br /><br />The only true new material heard in here is Catwoman's Theme (Mind If I CutIn) and Bane's Theme (Gotham's Reckoning) and a very uplifting, yet short, track entitled \\"Why Do We Fall?\\". The BIG track of the score is \\"Imagine the Fire\\" were Hans Zimmer is able to capture the final confrontation between Bane and Bruce Wayne. This cue, along with \\"Gotham's Reckoning\\" and \\"Why Do We Fall\\" are the true highlights because they sound fresh and, at the same time, familiar with the Batman world. But we are kept waiting for a light at the end of the darkness and the light is brought upon us with \\"Rise\\", only this time it doesn't sound refreshing.<br /><br />Howard Shore wrote, on Return of the King, that he read a message while having lunch on those final days of recording. It said: \\"It is good to begin well, it is better to end well.\\"<br /><br />I just feel Hans Zimmer could have ended things better, especially for the conclusion of this amazing story.

B000G8NXYG|4.0|Almost everyone gives this excellent film five stars therefore I'm giving it 4 stars.  The reason for this is the over-the-top histrionics characteristic of much of the dialogue.  I know.  I know.  I know.  This is the way the Japanese behave when confronted by stress.  Nonsense.  The Japanese are no more emotive than anybody else.  So what's the explanation?  My guess is that it is a Japanese theatrical convention.  Surely, the Japanese couldn't have achieved major success in business [or war for that matter], if they were so incredibly undisciplined.  At the same time, it is possible that such a convention is obvious because it is foreign.  Very likely American films have their own crazy conventions but, because we have watched U.S. films for so long, the unrealities of U.S. films have become invisible to us.<br /><br />Still, subtracting this emotiveness, this is an exceptional film on many levels.  Given the multiple reviews by critics more gifted than I, I will mention only one fascinating moment.  The very willing girl is 'dishonored' by the young samurai and her father goes ape.  At one point, a villager tries to calm him down by saying, \\"It's not like she was taken by a bandit.\\"  The implication is that unwilling rape is more dishonorable than consensual sex.  Interesting.

Overall, the book is a dark, original and thoroughly entertaining read.  I would highly recommend it.

I cannot stress the previous statement enough because I have read many of the reviews of this book including the negative ones (the one and two star reviews seemed as if they were written by people with more experience than a beginner.  For example one of them states that the author's opinion of depth field is wrong, which I'm not sure many beginners would know enough about photography to make such a statement.)  If you are a beginner, and you have absolutely no clue on how to manually adjust your camera's settings so that you can consistently capture a \\"correct exposure,\\" you need to buy this book!<br /><br />I also must stress that this book is not about creating beautiful compositions or how to post-process photographs. The author, Bryan Peterson, gives you the tools you need to take correctly exposed photographs (he even gives some tips on choosing the \\"creatively correct exposure\\" out of the many exposure options for a given shot).  However, he leaves the composition of the photo up to you, the photographer, which in my mind is the way it should be.  From the many professionals, amateurs, and seasoned hobbyists that I have spoken to, you will gradually develop an eye for photography as you become more experienced, which doesn't sound like something that you can learn from a book in my opinion.  If you were looking for a complete guide to becoming a photographer, this book is not it; however, I strongly recommend it as a tool to help you get there.<br /><br />--REVIEW--<br /><br />I've been easing myself into the world of photography for a little over a year now, and I have purchased multiple books about photography, but none of them have covered the subject of exposure in the simple and understandable way Bryan does.<br /><br />Mostbooks I have read speak about the exposure triangle and how each element has an impact on the exposure, but I can honestly say that I was still guessing after reading those books, and when I was out shooting, I still relied heavily on my camera presets. B007QWF26C|5.0|[[ASIN:B007QWF26C Blade II]]<br /><br />Year:  2002   Rating:  R   Runtime: 1 HR 57 MIN<br /><br />Aspect Ratio: 1:78.1   Video Resolution: 1080P<br />Audio: Eng 7.1 DTS-HD MA, Eng 5.1 DTS-HD MA, assorted foreign audio tracks  Subtitles: English SDH, Spanish, Portuguese, Japanese, Czech, Russian<br /><br />Director:  Guillermo del Toro<br />Writer:  David S. Goyer<br />Starring:  Wesley Snipes, Luke Gross, Kris Kristofferson, Leonor Varela, Norman Reedus, Thomas Kretschmann, and Ron Perlman<br /><br />Film: 4/5<br />Video: 4/5<br />Audio: 5/5<br />Extras: 4/5<br />Overall: 4/5<br /><br />A new form of vampire called Reapers are desolating the vampire population throughout Eastern Europe.  In a desperate attempt to stop this seemingly impossible foe the vampire nation enlists Blade to try and stop them.  He is given command of the Bloodpack, a specialized group of vampire commandos that were actually trained to kill Blade.  The Reapers put the uneasy partnership between Blade and the Bloodpack to the test as they prove to be as resilient as they are bloodthirsty with a secret agenda all their own.<br /><br />[[ASIN:B007QWF26C Blade II]] in some ways is a superior film to the original while in other ways it falls well short.  The main villain, Nomak (Luke Goss) is nowhere near as much fun or deliciously evil as Deacon Frost.  Nomak is setup as a bloodthirsty monster but by the end he's more of a tragic victim which robs the film of a certain amount of viciousness.  Where Blade II excels past its predecessor is in terms of the rest of the cast, its special effects, and direction.  Wesley Snipes and Kris Kristofferson return but it's the addition of the great Ron Pearlman that really pushes Blade II to the next level.  Also joining the cast is Norman Reedus (The Walking Dead), Matt Schulze (Fast Five), and Thomas Kretschmann (Wanted), they're not all names you'll recognize but you'll definitely know their faces.<br /><br />The believability of CGI made huge strides between the first and second Blade films.  There are still some questionable CGI in Blade II but nothing that really takes you out of the flow of the film.  Mexican director Guillermo del Toro brings an even bleaker and darker style to the franchise going as far as changing the aspect ratio from 2.35:1 to 1.85:1.  Guillermo del Toro's employs a much more fluid camera work than what was seen in the original and really tries to envelope you in the action.<br /><br />The stylistic changes made by del Toro don't always lend itself to making a great looking Blu-ray transfer.  If you watched Blade and Blade II back-to-back you might initially think that Blade has a superior transfer but you'd be wrong.  The transfer of Blade II is just as good as the one found on Blade it's just that Blade II is shot much differently than the original.  Blade II is a softer looking film than its predecessor; it's also much darker with long periods of the film taking place in gritty and murky sewers.  Does that type of environment lend itself well to HD?  Not really, but it was an intentional choice by the director and his cinematographer and this Blu-ray is a good representation of the how Blade II looked theatrically.  Colors, particularly reds pop very well, the grain is appropriate, and detail especially on close-ups is very good.  Black levels can be a little too dark at times leading to some information loss but that again has more to do with the stylistic choice of the filmmakers than the Blu-ray transfer.  I'd rather have the studio do nothing than overzealously tinker with the picture leading to too much digital manipulation.<br /><br />I thought the original Blade sounded spectacular but Blade II is even better.  The 7.1 DTS-HD MA track is easily demo material for anyone interested in giving their setup a thorough workout from head to toe.  Dialogue is firmly centered while the three front channels make slicing and dicing through the Reapers sound easy as pie (or should I say `humans'?).  If you're lucky enough to have a 7.1 setup the added surround channels don't go to waste as they all are given plenty to do while not sounding gimmicky in the process.  The LFE channel will give your downstairs neighbors reason to come knocking on your door since they'll think their ceiling is probably about to cave in.  It's a great sounding disc especially if you like listening to a rib cage break apart in graphic audio detail.<br /><br />The commentary tracks, deleted scenes, music videos, trailers, assorted featurettes, and The Blood Pact documentary are all ported over from the 2002 DVD release.  A new HD Director's Notebook and interview between Guillermo del Toro and writer David S. Goyer called Blade II: Blood Brothers is also included.  Pretty much everything you'd ever want to know about Blade II can be found here in one way or another.  Not all the material has aged well and you might get a sense of d&eacute;j&agrave; vu since some material is redundant but it's still a very decent supplement package even if most of it is showcased in SD.<br /><br />[[ASIN:B007QWF26C Blade II]], like the original Blade is still a silly karate-vampire flick but it delivers on what it advertises.  The Blu-ray presentation is strong across the board; the video might not blow you away but it is a good representation of the source material, the audio is second to none, and the special features have some new HD material added to an already informative package.  If you're a fan of Blade II this is really a no brainer, if you're a newcomer to the world of Blade I still recommend it as a blind buy.'?). B008645YEE|1.0|I may not be a musical scholar of any sorts, but I sense plenty wrong with this soundtrack and I'll voice my issues as best I can while not savvy in musical terminology. I may do future edits when I can word my opinions better, but for now endure my rant.<br /><br />My basic thoughts on soundtracks for Nolan's Batman films.(Might write their reviews one day)<br />Batman Begins: Intriguing enough, establishes the mystery and heroism decently with a dash of mysticism that actually works swell. Weak 4 stars.<br />The Dark Knight: More obnoxious and redundant, but still some listenable highlights. Strong 2 stars.<br /><br />But this, this is last straw. Most of this is mindless raves about how it brings things to a conclusion. I don't see how, if it didn't really go much anywhere to begin with. This could deserve two-stars at best, but that's too generous.<br /><br />Nearly all of it is just copy and paste from the last two soundtracks. Repackage them with sound editing to supposedly make it a refreshing experience. What may sound 'new' on the album, is actually the same what you heard from the last films only being released now. TDK OST suffered from this, but this running out of presents so instead put more boxes in boxes to fill the void.<br /><br />It's not a matter of whether anything you'd find whether \\"Bonus\\" or \\"Unreleased\\", it's just more Zimmer dicking around saying there's more if you search harder, while there isn't. \\"No Stone Unturned\\" for example. Can't I just go back and shuffle through the last two OSTs? I can find them still the same as usual rather than buy exact replicas all over again.<br /><br />\\"Storm is Coming\\" & \\"Death by Exile\\" - Totally WORTHLESS. Just 30 of seconds effects to segue into the next track. Anyone would sooner call this a sound-bridge used in film editing than anything musical.<br /><br />\\"On Thin Ice\\" - My God, a whole third addition note is actually hinted at here for Batman's ever so meaningful two-note piece. But just faintly here, the rest is just strings and we never hear of it again.<br /><br />\\"Mind if I Cut In\\" - When he does resort to instruments other than low strings(still haven't heard a woodwind from him in eons) he applies to the lowest denominator on trigger a audience response. Piano for instance can only serve two functions for Zimmer, tragedy or wittiness, there's no middle ground the keyboard is divided by only two.<br /><br />\\"Gotham's Reckoning\\" & \\"Fire Rises\\" & 'others I can't name right off' - A one note, one trick pony. Not only does this apply to only half of Bane's character, his brutality not his intelligence, his attempt to capture the militant masses goes full circle to capture none of it's essence. There's no variation where it could apply to each unique situation, the members growing by additional groups as the movie suggested, or the next plot point when the tone changes from foreboding to consuming. It ends the same as it began. That online crowd-sourcing was just a publicity stunt for Zimmer to gain more underserving hype. 'We'll fine tune it later,' Zimmer said. No sh*t, you muted anything unique or variant. You could have just gotten a dozen man's choir chanting on repeat and you'd get the same result you have.<br />Congratulations on a such a wasted opportunity.<br /><br />\\"Underground Army\\" - Just \\"And I Thought My Jokes were Bad\\". Doesn't matter is sound-editing made a certain 'umph' to it. Why am I listening to the same theme again if these new enemies have no connections with the Joker? Answer: Serve it's basic function as foreboding music to create the 'mood' and move on.<br /><br />\\"Why Do We Fall\\" & \\"Despair\\" - Advancing character and story moments, but all significant moments are irrelevantly the same according to Zimmer. Very basic tone of uplifting, as our heroes at their supposed lows rise again. These new, harder obstacles of broken Batman rising from the pit and returning to Gotham should be whole new experience. But, no Zimmer decides biggerand louder, because up the volume and bass is easier to convey 'triumphant' than writing additional material that could convey a sense of development and advancing of character and story.<br /><br />That's why stagnant mood music or constant ostinatos STIFLES progress. The characters may grow, change, and learn but the music sure IS NOT. And subconsciously that sterilizes the joy of the film even. When all three of the film's villains were defeated each film, who they are may have represented totally different themes in the batman, but Zimmer doesn't bother to make the distinction outside of 'this cue is when bad guy gets defeated... Wallah!' It's all the same to him and his indifference.<br /><br />\\"Rise\\" - The most insulting track of all. For the climax, the final decision and ultimate sacrifice for Gotham all that Zimmer did was just copy and paste parts of \\"I'm Not a Hero\\" & \\"The Dark Knight\\" from The Dark Knight OST and \\"Barbastella\\" from Batman Begins OST. Treat every plot point no matter how PIVOTAL to the story, is just going to be the SAME! Revisit the boy soprano to reflect the deaths of Wayne family is fine, but there is a difference between having parents slaughtered by a thief and a man sacrificing his life for the whole city. And that should be revisited with a varied emotion, not just apply the 'one size fits all' tragic sad boy singing solo.<br /><br />And that's my complaint with the Nolan's Batman in terms of music: Nothing is developed, it's just on repeat. Nothing grows, just tweaked a bit or made louder. Story and character cannot grow in an abyss, but Zimmer sure is reluctant to never have a solitary musical identity advance outside his fixed ostinato that would remain attached for a whole trilogy. I could go into greater detail how Star Wars Episodes 3 and 6 and LOTR: Return of the King trump anything Zimmer attempted but I'll just exhaust myself in this rant.<br /><br />He's not focused on a fully fledged human identity at all here. Just focusing on minimalistic motifs for a couple characters, and copies and pastes filler everywhere else to create moods to serve the scene to the lowest denominator.<br /><br />Well, I don't see costume or set designers stick to one color to create a 'mood', they'd be called lazy for not giving the material the attention to detail it deserves. Actors playing one-note performances serving solely to generate knee-jerk reactions would be criticized for not deserving an acting career. Imagine if he did mountain climbing disaster flick, he'll just sound edit an avalanche or gusts to make the 'mood'.<br /><br />This year we've been treated with three superhero films with varying soundtracks. \\"The Avengers\\" was average but served it's audience with bravado that heroes deserve. \\"Amazing Spiderman\\" was flawed, but accomplished duality of outrageous heroism and fragile humanity far better than Zimmer did with his hazy sound-scape you can't distinguish Batman or Bruce Wayne from it.<br />Even \\"Wrath of the Titans\\" that fell under the influence of Zimmer's suggested techniques is a joy to listen despite being a chaotic mess.

Dick short story, \\"We Can Remember it for you Wholesale,\\" and from there, there are a mixture of influences, the world in this \\"Recall\\" is clearly the \\"Blade Runner\\" world of Ridley Scott and weirdly enough there seem to be influences of \\"Star Wars: Revenge of the Sith.\\"<br /><br />This Recall focuses more on action, which has a scene in which Farrell and Beckinsale jump several floors of space, crash through numerous windows and land on what looks like very hard concrete slabs all without breaking any arms, legs or ribs. 0142419400|2.0|Warning: I hate to do this because I try NEVER to be spoilery but this review cannot be written without containing a few spoilers.<br /><br />Anna and the French Kiss on the surface is simple straightforward Chicklit. It's sweet and innocent with loveable characters and the backdrop of Paris. What more could you ask for? Unfortunately a lot.<br /><br />I really had a hard time liking either of the main characters. Anna seemed really shallow and whiney. She was all put out that she got to spend her entire senior year in Paris...THE Paris. She whined about it incessantly through the first chapters and then continued to whine about her father (who was very Nicholas Sparks-esque which did make me laugh a lot) for most of the story. And like omigosh, she only gets like just enough allowance to go to like 6 movies in one week. Like really? That's like so unfair, Dad. Hmmm...not really feeling it. To be fair, her parents did fight a lot and her dad is rather distant and like I said, Nicholas Sparks so a bit melodramtic but I felt like she was overdoing the teenage angst. The one thing I did like about her was her unwillingness to come between Ellie and St. Clair/Etienne. I admired her for that. It showed strength on her part and a true depth to her feelings for him. Wanting him to be happy and be his friend until he was willing to break it off with his girlfriend.<br /><br />Etienne/St. Clair is a super sweet boy. I loved the things he did for Anna and the way he treated her for the most part. They had an adorable relationship, very sweet and innocent. However, despite this he is overwhelmingly weak when it comes to relationships. This is the part that bugged me the most. He continually turns from his feelings for Anna and runs back to Ellie long after he should have ended it. He waits around for her to make HER feelings completely clear by forcing her to ask him to kiss her before finally ending a relationship that should have been over MONTHS ago.<br /><br />I could havemaybe overlooked this if he had come to her at the end and said in his polite British accent, \\"You know what, Ah-na, I've been a bloody wanker. Please forgive me.\\" But no, rather than taking the blame for it, he blames her for not telling him how she felt and trying to move on with other guys. HE HAD A GIRLFRIEND! She was not going to be THAT girl. Very frustrating and sort of ruined my view of him. I wanted to see a guy with some guts, who's willing to step out of a relationship just because it's not what's right for him, not stay in a relationship until he's sure he can jump right into another one because he's afraid of being alone. It really weakened his character for me and took away the depth of his feelings for Anna. If he really loved her that much, why would he stay with someone else?<br /><br />Also, the secondary characters, other than Rashmi and Amanda, were very wooden and flat. Rather than adding a lot to the story I felt like they were simply plot devices and didn't really help me to love the main characters. Etienne's bad relationship with his father could have been a lot stronger too. He was shown as controlling and there was some hinting at possible abuse but nothing concrete. He was a jerk for sure but didn't make me feel an intense hatred for him the way I have in other books. He just annoyed me and pointed out Etienne's weakness more than anything.<br /><br />The romance between Anna and Etienne/St. Clair was so beautiful. It was built gradually with the innocence and intensity of first love. Perkins captures that delicious feeling very realistically, however it is completely overshadowed for me by his tediously long relationship with Ellie and Etienne/St. Clair's weakness.<br /><br />In short, Anna and the French Kiss just didn't do it for me. I really did not enjoy this book. I hoped I would. I kept trying to, but honestly I just couldn't seem to figure out how to like it. I questioned this at first because it's very well written and the setting is gorgeous. But it all boiled down to the actual story and it's characters. For me, that's not a fun read.

0736621458|4.0|\\"Are there no pleasant things to speak of?...Do we have to talk always of horrors?\\" asks Maria at one point in For Whom the Bell Tolls.<br /><br />Good question.<br /><br />To be fair, For Whom the Bell Tolls isn't ALL about horrors.  It even has some pleasant moments.  But ultimately, it's about the selfless nature of war---which, though Hemingway clearly intends us to admire the acts of sacrifice to which the war incites his characters, I think is the greatest condemnation of war.<br /><br />But Hemingway's portrayal of this theme is quite powerful.  He isn't always consistent, but he is about as consistent as it is possible to be about such a theme and much more so than most, which is of great artistic value.<br /><br />It's also generally very well written, much more so than (and something of a relief after reading) a lot of faux-Hemingway like John Steinbeck or Cormac McCarthy.  And I thought this was much better than the only other Hemingway I've read, A Farewell to Arms.  But there are a few passages that miss the mark, such as this almost comically bad sex scene: \\"...They were having now and before and always and now and now and now. Oh, now, now, now, the only now, and above all now, and there is no other now but thou now and now is thy prophet. Now and forever now. Come now, now, for there is no now but now. Yes, now. Muck their egotism and their selfishness and their selfishness and their egotism and their conceit and their treachery. Muck them to hell and always. Muck them before we die for them. Muck them after we die for them. Muck them to death and hell...\\"  It goes on like this at some length.<br /><br />But in the end, Hemingway affirms that there are \\"pleasant things to speak of\\": \\"That is in Madrid. Just over the hills there, and down across the plain. Down out of the gray rocks and the pines, the heather and the gorse, across the yellow high plateau you see it rising white and beautiful. That part is just as true as Pilar's old women drinking the blood down at the slaughterhouse. There's no one thing that's true. It's all true. The way the planes are beautiful whether they are ours or theirs.\\"  But the horrors win out in the end: \\"The hell they are, he thought.\\"<br /><br />Three and a half stars.r /><br />The mind-numbing repetitiousness of this \\"description\\" (if one can call it that) is especially unfortunate as it echoes another passage just a few pages earlier which is intended to have quite a different feel: \\"...muck this whole treacherous muckfaced mucking country and every mucking Spaniard in it on either side and to hell forever. B00004VW35|4.0|Neil Young has always sneaked dollops of the country sound in his music, but \\"Old Ways\\" is one of the times he went all the way. Even Willie Nelson and Waylon Jennings showed up. Released during his highly experimental phase in the 1980s with Geffen records, fans probably shurgged it off at the time as \\"another messy miss.\\" At the time, Neil was trying the patience of his record label, and possibly some fans, with the wild synth rock of \\"Trans\\" followed up with the incredibly short \\"Everybody's Rockin'\\" - in two short years he leapt from Kraftwerk to Buddy Holly. The man definitely has range. But the precedents set by the previous two Geffen releases may have provided the psychological push for many fans to smear \\"Old Ways\\" as fetid tripe and not give it a second listen. Some may have stayed away simply due to the \\"country\\" label.<br /><br />That's all too bad, because \\"Old Ways\\" includes some great country songs that show an absolute mastery of the form. Stunning billowing harmonica opens the record like a cool breeze through an old willow tree. It's one of the most longing and lonely sounds Young has crafted. Flying Dutchman strings then punctuate the chorus. This track definitely sits comfortably beside Young's best covers. Then the the cattle prod comes out for \\"Back to the Country,\\" to which only people with their feet encased in cement wouldn't dance to. Willie Nelson saddles up for the next number, \\"Are There Any More Real Cowboys,\\" which could have easily played on any 1980s country station. And it's really fun to hear Nelson sing \\"not the one that's snortin' cocaine when the honky-tonks all closed\\" with utter conviction. Next, a classic country ballad, sung to a dream woman, slows down the pace and allows for a slow dance. Press her close, now.<br /><br />Nearly every Young album contains some oddity, but \\"Old Ways\\" hides one of the classics: \\"Misfits\\" isn't really a country song... it's... umm... not a country song. Young sings of \\"space stations,\\" \\"a see-thru' hooker\\" and \\"elephantine.\\" It starts with a simple rhythmic thud and crescendos with strings and wailing voices that shower over the mix like distant fireworks. The song may have fit better on an album such as 1987's \\"Life,\\" but here it definitely provides variety and color. It's probably one of his most underrated obscure songs. A live performance of \\"California Sunset,\\" once the first song on side two, again boosts the mood with a fervent ode to the golden state. The crowd seems to agree. The title track exudes the classic sloppy and irresistible country sound that remains one of Young's most endearing trademarks. Young is definitely not set in his ways, apart from his defiance of record label demands. And one more country superstar joins Young on \\"Bound For Glory,\\" another song which probably should have sat high on the country charts. Waylon Jennings and Young sing about passionate vagabonds on a mission and who were \\"bound for each other.\\"<br /><br />\\"Old ways\\" probably plays better today than in 1985 because Neil has made astonishing comebacks since those murky Geffen days. Some at the time felt his career was over. Far from it. It was just one more phase in a career that still remains undefinable. And though he recently sang that his \\"sales have tanked\\" he seems to keep going nonetheless. \\"Old Ways,\\" though probably not for everyone, remains a testament to the fascinating career of a musician who seems to be able to pull off just about anything. When's the qawwali album?thru' hooker\\" and \\"elephantine.\\" It starts with a simple rhythmic thud and crescendos with strings and wailing voices that shower over the mix like distant fireworks.
