B004PGNB1K|3.0|I have read the Name of the Wind about 4 times now, and I just finished the second reading of The Wise Man's Fear.<br />Here are my impressions about the Wise Man's Fear.<br />First of all, it is an enjoyable tale, but not as consistent or well written as it could have been. The hero, Kvothe, continues the story of him growing up. At the end of the book, I could not help having mixed feeling about him.<br /><br />First of all, about the writing style. Mr Rothfuss is a great writer, but I feel his books may suffer from the pressure to publish imposed on him by the publisher. Mr Rothfuss needs time, just like a good wine. Give him time to polish and you will have a great book. Hurry him and you will end up with a nice story. In The Wise Man's Fear, descriptions of scenes, impressions and actions are almost always done using the adjective \\"sharp\\": \\"his eyes were sharp and bright\\", \\"sharp as shattered glass\\", \\"sharp and strong and sweet\\", \\"sharp as early morning birdsong\\", \\"sharp breath\\", \\"every sharp and painful thing\\", \\"sharp as a mouthful of boken glass\\", \\"sharp crack of splintering wood\\"...Sometimes it is ok and conveys well the impressions of the character but after some time it feels a little bit overused and repetitive. I would have liked to read a more varied Rothfussian style.<br /><br />Some side-stories in the book I could not really care for. The whole Felurian ark, in which Kvothe learn uninhibited sex from a sex god and thereafter becomes a pornstar with University girls, did not contribute to building him as a wothwhile character. The endless descriptions of kama-sutra style techniques was quite heavy handed.<br /><br />About the hero himself, I feel a bit confused. We are told many times he behaves older than he looks, but his actions, especially in the Wise Man's Fear, are incredibly puerile. He learns everything quickly but he never grows wiser or more patient. He has a inflated sense of his own importance and likes spreading rumours about himself. Moreover, in the few sections of the book told in the present, in which the hero is adult and tells the story of his life, Kvothe the hero always end up criticizing somebody, almost insulting at times , as if he knew that he so much better than anybody. Kvothe seems to be a condescending snob. At the end of the Wise Man's Fear, I liked the hero better as the kind, unassuming and efficient inkeeper. If this was Mr Rothfuss's intention, then I need to increase my 3-star rating. At the moment, I am just confused. I hope Kvothe grows up to be a responsible young adult in the next book.

Whereas the focus of the Percy Jackson series was Greek Mythology, this new series (alluded to in the Jackson series) focuses on the children of the Roman gods and their offspring at a camp similar to Camp Half-Blood. \\"The new cast features Jason, Piper, and Leo, teen demigods who are just coming to understand and use their unique abilities as they learn how much depends upon their wits, courage, and fast-developing friendship.\\"  There are some of our \\"former friends\\" in this series as well. Their parents are gods in their Roman rather than Greek personae. And, as in the other books, the Quest: by sunset of the solstice in three days, the teens must rescue Hera, Queen of the gods, otherwise the giant king created to destroy Zeus and unseat the gods of Olympus, will rise. Their quest takes them across the United States, sometimes flying on a mechanical, 60-foot dragon. This is an amazingly creative and enjoyable book; my thanks to Rick Riordan for making another wonderful series that will feed my addiction for mythology!

B006JD48NM|5.0|This outstanding bio-pic about the life of three-times Formula One World Champion Ayrton Senna is an exemplary textbook study in documentary film-making. Regardless of whether or not you have any interest in motor racing or in Senna himself, this film really is one of the finest-ever examples of the genre you're ever likely to see.<br /><br />All F1 fans know that Senna (full name Ayrton Senna de Silva) died on 1st May 1994 following a fatal crash in the Italian Grand Prix at San Morino, the last driver (to date, August 2012) to die in a F1 racing accident. The film pieces together archive footage tracing early life and go-karting success, obsession with driving and winning and later days of international super-stardom of this most talented and fearless driver. His career-long rivalry with cool-as-ice French driver Alain Prost is revealed in a direct and no-holds barred narrative containing some astounding unguarded, off-the-record moments of interaction between these two individuals of contrasting (and clashing) temperaments.<br /><br />Senna's unique driving talent and personal modesty, the enormous impact his success had on popular sentiment in Brazil, his heartfelt religious convictions and the work and money he dedicated to the education of under-privileged children in his native land are all revealed. On the way we are shown rare peeks into confrontations at F1 pre-race drivers' meetings, and some of the F1 engineering innovations which made their way into mass-production vehicles - like traction control for example.<br /><br />Overall this is a gripping, well-paced film which holds the attention throughout with never a dull moment. The choice to forego a voiceover-narrator and allow the archive film to speak for itself makes the film's impact on the viewer  all the more powerful. Some of the driver's-eye-from-the-car camera-work during F1 races is genuinely edge-of-the-seat terrifying, and allows you to appreciate just what phenomenal speeds were and are involved.<br /><br />If you appreciate good film-making with fine editing, regardless of the subject matter, you really should see this. If you are a student of film or aspiring film-maker then seeing and studying `Senna' - several times, not just once - is a must, and if you're a F1 fan you are gonna love it.<br /><br />Five stars for excellence.lved.<br /><br />If you appreciate good film-making with fine editing, regardless of the subject matter, you really should see this. B002R27J1W|5.0|Adapted from a play for the screen, Wait Until Dark (1967) is highly effective suspense thriller, that owes much of its success to a solid story, a quick pace, and some very strong acting performances.  Most of the film takes place in a basement apartment in New York City, where a blind woman played by Audrey Hepburn ([[ASIN:B006TTC5WC Charade]], [[ASIN:B001UHOWW4 Breakfast at Tiffany's]])  becomes involved in a web of intrigue, surrounding a shipment of drugs.  She will need to draw upon all her wits and strength to survive a night of sheer terror.  Some spoilers follow.<br /><br />Afraid that it will be stolen, a woman smuggling a doll stuffed with heroin on a flight from Canada into New York, passes the doll to a stranger, Sam Hendrix (Efrem Zimbalist Jr.) a photographer.  Hendrix takes the doll home to his apartment, where he lives with his wife Suzy (Hepburn), who is blind.<br /><br />A criminal named Harry Roat (Alan Arkin), gets conmen Mike Talman (Richard Crenna) and Carlino (Jack Weston) involved in an elaborate scheme to try and trick Suzy into giving them doll.  The criminals first get Sam out of the way, and then Talman moves in pretending to be an old army buddy.  Carlino plays a cop investigating a murder, while Roat plays a variety of characters.  With Sam gone, Suzy's only ally is Gloria (Julie Herrod), a teenage girl who lives upstairs.<br /><br />Audrey Hepburn's performance is simply marvelous, ranging from bubbly and full of emotion to absolute terror.  Suzy is remarkably capable and intelligent, and Hepburn is very convincing playing a person unable to see.  When things turn dark and dangerous, Hepburn makes you feel the chill of Suzy's fear and panic, with Henry Mancini's fantastic score also contributing to heightening the drama.<br /><br />Alan Arkin (Inspector Clouseau, Catch 22) may have been known for quirky comedic roles, and while he displays some comedic talent in one of his various guises, he is truly chilling playing a ruthless criminal.  Richard Crenna's performance is so good that even though you know Mike is a bad guy, you wish he wasn't.  Jack Weston ([[ASIN:B007VFF7JQ Incredible Mr. Limpet]]) might be more familiar in lighter roles often playing the loyal friend.  Going a bit against type, Weston doesn't quite fit as a heavy, but is still fun to watch.  Although Gloria starts out as a bratty kid, she becomes a key character later.  Julie Herrod's performance may not be the most polished, but it adds a certain sense of realism to the drama.  Efrem Zimbalist Jr. isn't in the film very much, but is a steady presence when he is.<br /><br />Although some elements from the play may not translate seamlessly to the film adaptation, the story holds together well and flows nicely, under the direction of Terence Young ([[ASIN:B001AQO3U6 Dr. No]], [[ASIN:B001AQO3WO From Russia with Love]], [[ASIN:B001AQO45K Thunderball]]).  From early on, it is clear that Suzy is very vulnerable and in danger.  This situation, along with Audrey Hepburn's animated performance, draws your attention to Suzy, and the superior performances of the rest of the cast, make the world inside the apartment seem real and intimate.  Even if you are familiar with the story, Wait Until Dark still remains very compelling and suspenseful.  There are some issues, but it's easy to ignore them and just enjoy this great thriller.al. B004Q3QRWC|5.0|A Visit from the Good Squad is a novel that reads like a collection of short stories. The narrative of the stories is loosely connected by a pool of characters that span the 60s to somewhere in the near future. What is brilliant about this book for me is the way it approximates real life in the way everyone is a main character of his or her own story.<br /><br />Characters who appear as the main character in one story emerges as a supporting one in another and it is with a start sometimes when you encounter the same character years later and he or she is almost unrecognizable, like the young rakish rock musician who ends up old and forgotten. The ravages of time is a central theme in these narratives just as Bennie, a music producer and failed guitarist himself, tries to persuade his old friend and never-made-it rock star Scott to take the stage: \\"You gonna let that goon push you around?\\", and Scott answers \\"The goon won\\".<br /><br />These characters are all terribly flawed: a kleptomaniac who steals indiscriminately from her dates and plumber, a journalist who sexually assaults a young female actress during an interview, a disgraced publicist who works for a dictator against her better conscience, and a professor who is sent to Italy to look for a missing teenage niece, but is more interested in visiting art museums and escape from his home responsibilities on the funds given by his sister and husband, are just some of them. However with Egan's sympathetic brushstrokes, they inevitably find that moment of grace, which is sometimes all that is needed to turn things around. Even if things never do, like for the suicidal character who drowns, we learn not to make snap judgements about people and the decisions they make.

0062119044|5.0|I have difficulty imagining that anyone who has read this book would honestly say that he \\"enjoyed\\" it. Lionel Shriver didn't write We Need to Talk About Kevin for anything as frivolous as readers' enjoyment; her purpose was much greater. In fact, We Need to Talk About Kevin is painful, disturbing, and literally gut-wrenching: I could only read it in small gulps and continue to be affected by the emotional trauma of its final chapters.<br /><br />But I cannot recall the last time I read a more important novel or one that expected so much of me at a human (and humane) level. If the reader is willing to allow the author inside to fiddle around with whatever concepts he or she may have of morality, justice, guilt, and parental responsibility in the face of the heinous acts of violence that have become as commonplace (and, in some ways, as remote) as typhoons in the western Pacific, be warned that Shriver leaves marks.<br /><br />To say Shriver is articulate and eloquent is almost to damn her with faint praise; We Need to Talk About Kevin is a masterwork of language, at the level of word choice and sentence structure, certainly, but also in Shriver's ability to sense when terse aphorism is appropriate and when nothing less than the lush, graphic, deliberate inducement of pain will do. Of certain books I've sometimes said, damningly, that they are more interesting to talk about than to read; this one is engrossing to read and demands to be talked about. I'd go so far as to recommend that you read it with a friend or as part of a book group: Afterwards, you will want to talk, if only for catharsis, if only to try to realign your certainties.<br /><br />At a time in our cultural (de)evolution when complex moral questions are repeatedly reduced to stick-figure epigrams and when the intellectual capacity to contain paradox has very nearly become vestigial, Shriver asks the reader to reanimate his ability to sit still and contemplate the depths of human emotional entanglement, to tolerate appalling contradiction. I happened to read this book just a few weeks after the shooting tragedy in Aurora, Colorado and directly after the massacre at the Sikh temple in Oak Grove, WI. The media - and the curse/blessing of social media - are still falling all over their own feet to pose (and then to answer, in 30-second sound bites) the question: Why? If you're satisfied with bromides like \\"violent video games,\\" \\"lowered moral standards,\\" \\"access to guns,\\" or \\"atheism,\\" do not read this book. It contains nothing that will make you feel better. To Shriver's eternal credit, making us feel better was the last thing she had in mind.

The author admits that there is no vitamin B12 in non animal food, and that is the one supplement he recommends. Would this be the case in nature if we were not meant to eat any animal protein?<br /><br />This may indeed be a diet that is healthy, but to change over to these recommendations would be to eat the opposite of what I've been living on. I may just try it one day. It seems so drastic and I wonder if eliminating all animal products helps you lose weight simply because of the number of calories and the amount of fat.To me it does not make sense scientifically - I've experienced weight loss from a low carb diet and weight gain from adding carbs. But I've never tried a totally vegan diet.<br /><br />He praises Dr. Swank's low fat diet, whereas my husband tried this when he was diagnosed with MS in 1975, and it did not help him and he was unable to continue very long on it. In the past few years I have been seeing more about the need for fat (now called \\"good fats\\") in the human diet; the low fat craze of the 60s and 70s is no longer in favour. The need for Omega 3 because we get too much Omega 6 was only discovered in the 1980s and now I thought it was universally accepted.<br /><br />Anyway, to wrap this up, this is a startling book. It's worth reading, but it may be very difficult to actually follow the advice. I do want to try it though, to see if I could really lose weight eating mashed potatoes, beans and corn, as long as I don't eat meat, eggs or dairy. I just don't think I could stay on it for long, and I'd be worried about giving up all animal protein.

0545091020|2.0|This review is for the audiobook version - enough people are here to tell you what the book is like. In fact, I quite enjoy the story. It has some genre-standard elements and the first person present-tense narration can be irritating, but it goes cool places and is worth a shot for kids and adults alike.<br /><br />The audiobook, however, should be avoided. Oh, wow, is it bad. Carolyn McCormick is the reader, and honestly, I always thought she was a good actor. I've seen her in almost a hundred Law & Order episodes and thought her performance was pretty good in every one.<br /><br />But her reading of this book is just... dead. Sometimes you get audiobooks performed by people who mispronounce words or give characters strange voices, but at least they can usually bring some enthusiasm with them. McCormick doesn't. She is monotone and lifeless, even at dramatic moments. It's especially bad as the story is related in the first-person; the lack of inflection makes it sound like Katniss doesn't care when people die, or when she's in danger herself. It also never, ever sounds like a teenage girl is talking, you can't even imagine it with this voice.<br /><br />Worse still is the dialogue. She barely does any voices, except to change pitch slightly. This actually becomes a problem when she has to read a male character, who will invariably sound like a lummox with a deep voice and no brains.<br /><br />If you were thinking about buying the audiobook instead of the text edition, give it a pass. The story will be much more effective for you without this lifeless performance.

0307719219|4.0|I give this book 4 stars only because of a few things the authors left out, in my opinion.<br /><br />This is an excellent and thought-provoking read and highly recommended for everyone, especially many modern politicians (even a certain president), who can't see that their \\"political institutions\\" are actually impeding progress and allowing poverty to continue in their countries.<br /><br />IMO, what they left out is why certain groups, like the Pilgrims, et al., refused to lived in an \\"extractive\\" society. What in their past made them behave like that?<br /><br />Also, as others have written, they didn't really talk about the role of education in determining whether people would accept an extractive society or not.<br /><br />The influence of religion (particularly, Christianity) on the formation of many inclusive nations was not discussed.<br /><br />The authors somewhat discounted the effect that help by America had on certain nations, e.g., South Korea, Japan, Germany, etc. For example, would Japan have continued to be an extractive nation if it had won WWII, and not received rebuilding help from the U.S.?<br /><br />My mom and I both read this book and enjoyed it immensely. We had several slight disagreements with the initial conclusions of why nations fail but overall, agreed with the authors' opinions.<br /><br />A brilliant point the authors made is that the elite rulers of 3rd world nations DON'T want their nations to succeed on the level of 1st world nations since in doing so, they would lose their power, or the mass of \\"happy\\" workers would lose their jobs through \\"creative destruction.\\"  I guess I always knew this but had never heard it explained so well, and with so many good examples. I saw this firsthand when I lived in Central America.<br /><br />Finally, this book reminded me of Ayn Rand's \\"Atlas Shrugged,\\" another book everyone should read. Rand's depiction of \\"Producers & Looters,\\" or politicians who create ridiculous institutions, or barriers to capitalism, sounded very familiar to what the authors of this book had to say. I bet they would mostly agree on why nations fail.capitalism, sounded very familiar to what the authors of this book had to say. I bet they would mostly agree on why nations fail.
